B00ABKGG0C|4.0|In Blood Song, Anthony Ryan writes of Vaelin's growth from a child left by his father to be raised by an order of fighting monks into a man upon whom world events turn.<br /><br />Why to buy this book: After I got past the prologue, most of this book was incredibly good.  As in the writing ranked up there with my favorite fantasy series of all time.  The author does a fantastic job of throwing you inside the head of the character.  I found it agonizing each time life intruded in such a way that it forced me to put the book down.<br /><br />Why not to buy the book: The prologue sets up the ending of the book from the POV of one of the protagonist's enemies.  From that character's perspective, what the protagonist does at the end was a terrible tragedy of epic proportion.  When we get to the event from the protagonist's POV, the epic event was just a small part of the battle, making the entire thing seem anticlimactic.  Overall, in fact, the ending of the book feels rushed and unsatisfying considering the awesomeness of the buildup.  Additionally, some idiot made the decision that the prologue and each interlude between parts should be printed in all italics.  To be fair, maybe the interludes added something to the story that I missed because, quite frankly, if you're going to write page after page in italics, I assume you have no desire for me to actually read it.  I ended up skipping all the interludes.  That brings me back to the really crappy prologue: besides being seemingly endless and written is a visually offensive font, it's boring and slow, and I didn't find the POV character relatable in any way.  Despite spending my hard-earned money on the novel, I almost gave up reading it when I looked ahead to find I was only halfway through the atrocious beginning.  I think that, overall, I would have enjoyed the book much better had I simply skipped reading the prologue.<br /><br />Bottom Line: I went off on the book a bit in the previous section, and, judging from all the 5 star reviews the book has received, I think a lot of people will disagree with my nitpicking.  To those people, please try to understand my disappointment.  With the well-noted exception of the prologue, two thirds through this book, I thought, \\"I've found a new book to add to my favorites of all time!\\"  Instead, I'm left with, \\"I strongly recommend it, but...\\"  4 stars.

B00CRW2066|3.0|\\"Trouble Will Find Me\\", the eighth studio album from the Brooklyn based The National, is beautiful and melancholy. The theme that you have to live life, rather than read about it, is present throughout the album especially on the bitter track \\"Graceless\\" and the timeless \\"Fireproof\\". \\"Don't Swallow the Cap\\", the album's most memorable track, offers an upbeat and defiant tone, as Berninger proclaims \\"If you want to see me cry/play \\"Let It Be\\" or \\"Nevermind\\".<br /><br />The difference between \\"Trouble Will Find Me\\" and an album like Nirvana's \\"Nevermind\\" is range. \\"Nevermind\\" has range of emotion, range of tone and most importantly musical range. The National strike one chord and they stick to it. This would be a great album if you have just ended a relationship or someone close to you has died, but for listeners who are getting ready for sunshine and summertime, it feels long and exhausting.<br /><br />The National are good at what they do, writing sorrowful songs that can bring emotion out of the coldest of hearts. \\"Trouble Will Find Me\\" is skillful and stylish making it perfect for a raining day but it's the sunny, happier times where you might want put it away and to listen to some Daft Punk instead. http://veggiefansmusic.blogspot.com/

0307950689|3.0|The Lost Symbol is by no means a bad book by itself, but it doesn't really compare to the Robert Langdon stories that preceded it IMHO. To me it seems this book is trying to compete with other 'historical' stories, such as the National Treasure movies, and that's unfortunate considering Dan Brown made a name for himself within the historical/religious mysteries. The story for TLS is a genuinely interesting one, it's just the execution of it and anticlimactic ending that could've used some editing. Although this book is about five years old now I still feel the need to give a SPOILER warning:<br /><br />There was a significant amount of unnecessary information here that could've easily been omitted. If the dreck were removed the book probably could've been cut almost in half and the story would've been twice as captivating. Furthermore, most of the characters behaved either very stereotypically or quite ineptly; like they were just going through the motions. Even Langdon, who is supposed to be a brilliant professor and has been in more tense situations than those presented in this book, acted like a caricature much of the time. His reluctance to simply react to what's going on throughout most of the book slowed the pacing to the point where it became difficult to continue at times.<br /><br />While there were a number of questionable plot threads in TLS two things in particular stood out to me as just plain unimaginitive; which was the involvement of the CIA and the motivation of the antagonist. The villain records some public officials in some contextually embarrassing circumstances and black-mails the CIA into helping him. The problems this creates are manifold: First of all the CIA is neither law-enforcement nor military. Sure they participate in such matters on occasion, but they have no real investigative authority or jurisdiction beyond their primary duty of collecting and analyzing foreign intelligence. Secondly, no government agency is responsible for protecting the reputation of their officials, least of all the CIA. The information the villain threatened to release is also in no way a national security concern, and could be easily rationalized, so if Brown felt compelled to add another angle of suspense how about actually doing something useful out of the noetic science stuff he introduced and did nothing with?<br /><br />Then we come to the main antagonist of the story; whose identity was so obvious I stopped reading his chosen name about a quarter of the way through. The issue I had with him was that his whole vendetta could've been avoided had the events during his 18th birthday and inside the Turkish prison been written logically. Apparently, when a Solomon turns 18 he/she is given their portion of the family's inheritance, so he/she can choose their own path. However, the Freemasons' most valued artifact should have NOTHING to do with a family's inheritance, especially for someone who is both uninitiated and immature, no matter who the father is. Without knowing about the pyramid, Zach would've stayed 'dead'. I also doubt that his inheritance would be large enough for a couple years of debauchery in Europe before getting arrested, living very well in Greece for six years, and ten years forming his plan without a source of income. Then there's the extreme implausibility of him reaching the highest level of the Freemasons in just a few years, as well as having a prison cell within ear's shot of warden's office or even guard's desk.<br /><br />Despite all these criticisms, and many others I'll leave to my fellow commenters, this book was still an entertaining read, and it goes by very quickly once you get past the VERY slow start and are prepared for the dry, overlong ending following the climax of the story. If you enjoyed the first two Langdon adventures, or Dan Brown's novels in general, then chances are you'll like this one as well, but I recommend getting the book used or from the library.ing the reputation of their officials, least of all the CIA. B00C1N9JWO|5.0|~Reviewed by FRANCESCA & posted at Under the Covers Book Blog<br /><br />\\"I wish I could give this book 10 Stars!  Every single page of this book is packed with intensity.  It's enthralling, all consuming, and mind blowing.  Yes, I was thoroughly blown away! \\" ~Under the Covers<br /><br />Highly anticipated books like this always scare me.  What happens if the author can't take the pressure of writing such an anticipated story and it falls flat?  What if it doesn't go the way I WANT it to go?  There are no guarantees and I was both extremely excited and extremely scared to start reading.<br /><br />Another fear of mine was the fact that knowing this book had the big Ghost reveal, this story would be centered around the Psy.  In the past, the psy-only books have not been my favorites.  They lack some of the intensity that the changelings bring to the world.<br /><br />I shouldn't have worried.  I was hooked from the very first line.  I wrote my review with names in it and then have changed them to HERO and HEROINE in case people don't want to know.<br /><br />There are some series that always have a tough leader, a guy that makes those sometimes ruthless decisions that go against most morals.  He may seem heartless but there is always a purpose to his actions.  The last one I remember that caught my interest as much was Jonas from the Breeds series by Lora Leigh.  But after that, it's been the HERO.  The HERO, who should be psy and Silent, but has so much bottled up passion he could melt you at his feet.  The HERO, who is ruthless in the pursuit of what he wants and won't leave anything or anyone who gets in his path standing.<br /><br />Then focus all that intensity on the woman he loves.  OMG!!!!!  Ms. Singh, I am in awe of what you've done with this story.  From all the layers to the HERO we get to unfold, to the story of his one true love.  This is a second chance at love story.  The girl he fell in love with as a kid and was taken from him.  Brutally.  The one he's been searching for since.  For such a hard shell on the exterior, I couldn't help but melt at the softness inside of him.<br /><br />The HERO is, in one word, INTENSE.  There's so much he has learned to keep bottled up and as that shell cracks and all that intensity spills out..... Be prepared to fall in love.  Changelings have nothing on the HERO.<br /><br />The HEROINE was also perfect for him.  The perfect balance to his intensity.  Nurturing, soft, but strong at the core.  Able to stand on her own.  Able to fight for her man.  Able to heal him.<br /><br />Then there's the Ghost.  On this subject, I'm keeping my mouth shut.  However, I will say that I found the big reveal a bit....anticlimactic.  After waiting to find out who the Ghost is for so long, I was expecting bombs going off, cities collapsing and the Ghost riding in on some white horse to save the race.  I don't know.  I wanted something epic.  Instead, we find out as almost an afterthought.  And that's all I'm going to say.  No more.<br /><br />Nalini Singh's writing has the ability to paint a picture in my mind.  Beautifully crafted words that flow and let the imagination run wild.  And for the span of time it took me to read this book, I was living it.<br /><br />I wish I could give this book 10 Stars!  Every single page of this book is packed with intensity.  It's enthralling, all consuming, and mind blowing.  Yes, I was thoroughly blown away! 0312611692|3.0|This is a long winded, hard read.<br /><br />Morris is an archaeologist who takes the broader view that his field encourages, in trying to explain why it is that the west is more economically developed than the East in the early 21st century, but he also rambles a bit too much. With his archaeological background, he emphasises the role of geography in human development, something that might seem natural and obvious when one tries to determine why and where an old lost settlement sprang up, and what nearby trade and resources it might have had, and why it declined, etc etc. Highlighting the importance of geography in human history and development I think is the right angle, as in many historical analyses people's cultural ego makes them generally under-play and disregard something so seemingly unimportant as where you are and the shape of your landmass and how this affects who you are and how the history of your country develops. However, there are also some glaring weaknesses in Morris's general discussion.<br /><br />Here is one. In describing the origins of the Industrial Revolution in the 17th-19th centuries occurring in western Europe rather than the East(a key factor in the development of the modern world) he emphases geography and states that NW Europe, and in particular the UK, was better placed geographically to develop steam and coal, and kickstart the benefits of replacing human power with much more powerful steam and coal power. They were closest to the large and innovative market of the USA, and coal was local and plentiful (But then why didn't coal-rich USA develop steam power first?-no mention of this). Wages were high and labour was relatively scarce, so there was incentive to make machines to boost productivity, rather than just pay more low paid workers to work more. But Morris fails to really mention or highlight a major and key ingredient between western Europe and the East- a culture that rewarded innovation and incentive existed in the west, but not so much in the east. The east during the times of the 17th-19th centuries generally lacked such a culture of innovation (they still do, to a point). The reasons for this are many, but include:<br /><br />The cultural environment in the east has long fostered and enforced an existing, rigid social hierarchy, and this generally discourages lower and middle class innovation. In line with this is the general lack of/weak property and copyright laws. Any lower or middle class innovation was either stolen, or at best actively discouraged, by the elite because they generally don't want to reward the lower and middle classes, as this would under-mine their social power and control ('creative destruction'). They may even want to keep locals poor, despite rhetoric to the contrary. (Foreigners are also often restricted for the same reasons, the elite don't want them making too much money from the locals, and in particular, depriving the local elite of making this same money from them. That is why in Adam Smith's day, there were only 2 ports open for foreign ships in China, and even these were restrictive). Anything that is made, discovered, or found by locals, is generally supposed to be reserved for the elite under the existing strict social hierarchy, but the elite themselves generally won't invent something, because they already have large numbers of low paid workers to do all the work required for them, within a very stable social system.<br /><br />So what point is there to for anyone to innovate and develop a steam engine? If you do actually make something useful, the elite will either confiscate it or steal it, because it will either upset the existing social system, or much worse, if it is patented and marketed by the lower and middle classes, will give the lower and middle classes money and reward, rather than the elite themselves, which is an affront to their sense of pride and honour. So there is also weak copyright and property laws. So generally, nobody invents anything, or at best, such processes are much slower and weaker. So no steam engines invented in China, despite plentiful coal. So no industrial revolution.<br /><br />(Oh, and there is another thing Morris doesn't mention at all, particularly about Chinese coal. Chinese coal was generally a long way from ports, down rivers which were sometimes wild and unnavigable, unlike the situation in Britain. Coal is a bulk commodity and as such is very sensitive to transport costs. Transporting it economically in China from where it was, to ports and people was much more difficult and expensive. This is why there is STILL so much coal in Mongolia for example, more even than in Britain before the Industrial Revolution, but which has never been mined, because it was simply too far away from anything, but that is now gradually changing, with modern communication, transport and technology. )<br /><br />In other words, Britain had lots of cheap coal, that was also nearby, AND a market system that encouraged and rewarded innovation and development, without just stealing such for the benefit of the elite or the state. The East generally didn't have these things, so no Industrial revolution. He doesn't talk much about this major second part of the equation, but having lived and worked in Asian business for several years (within the realm of natural resources), it is a key factor STILL. There is still a sense in many places that the elite should have the rights to things they don't generally do anything to make, invent, or attain, which both discourages innovation amongst the lower and middle classes, as well as keep things under-developed, but this is slowly changing. In many places those who have the most money get to say how the law operates, or they simply buy-out smaller companies to keep them from developing too much, or they transfer ownership by nefarious methods so as to steal their hard earned assets for themselves (a 'takeover', but often without accompanying compensation to investors). As said, things are slowly changing, and of course it varies from place to place and business type, but the general business environment is in some places still a long way off what is generally considered 'standard' practice in the west.<br /><br />This may all sound culturally racist and patronising, and there is of course a balance to what foreigners, for example, should be allowed to do, but getting this balance right is complicated and difficult, and locals themselves may often feel the same way. Weak property and foreign investment laws are now better, but still echo what they were in Adam Smith's day, when he stated that a culture which only allows ships in only two of its many ports is not going to get the same amount of business (and social development) when compared to a culture that allows much freer and wider trade, in all of its' ports. It's simple mathematics and geography. I know of local business people in the east, for example, who say they one can't invest in local natural resource projects, for example, unless one is connected to government (the modern 'elite'), because otherwise the government will simply steal anything you find, with the government also believing that there is essentially little wrong with this. (An echo of the old cultural/ social mentality, that everything of value should be exclusively reserved for the 'elite'). I also know of local elites, who won't allow or strongly discourage development, research, or change of any kind, in areas they control, because it would undermine their considerable social authority. In other cases, development is allowed, but the only ones allowed to develop anything are the elite themselves, however they might not have the technology or assets to develop the things in question, so nothing eventuates. All this is hardly a recipe to encourage and reward the lower and middle classes, and encourage innovation, and the key factor is more often than not an existing, rigid social hierarchy, with the associated belief that the local elites are also the only ones allowed to do anything, including no-one in the lower or middle classes, the key drivers of social change and development. So it was at the time of the Industrial Revolution, no steam engines in China were the result, despite all their coal.n to investors). As said, things are slowly changing, and of course it varies from place to place and business type, but the general business environment is in some places still a long way off what is generally considered 'standard' practice in the west.<br /><br />This may all sound culturally racist and patronising, and there is of course a balance to what foreigners, for example, should be allowed to do, but getting this balance right is complicated and difficult, and locals themselves may often feel the same way. B00BEXB18M|4.0|Heavy metal fans seem to be the most conservative critics: Deviate from the tried-and-true formula for thrash/power/speed/etc. metal and your fans feel betrayed and turn their backs. Hey, people other than myself might begin to hear about this band, and even like them! That's selling out! Idiots who listen to mainstream music might discover my favorite band's back catalog, previously only known to me and a few select others!<br /><br />But I was reminded of two things while listening to Super Collider: First, Megadeth has never really been all about pure thrash metal. Sure, they have two of the greatest thrash albums of all time (Peace Sells, Rust in Peace), but in keeping up with Metallica, Megadeth was happy to abandon the cause and seek success elsewhere. As a result, they wrote a whole bunch of hummable, memorable, and yet still metal songs: Symphony of Destruction, Reckoning Day, Train of Consequences, Have Cool Will Travel, etc. Were these not better than any single song on So Far So Good... So What? Sure, Megadeth recorded duds, too, but Risk WAS risky (considering how much metalheads were always going to hate it), and it was anyway bookended by the very good Cryptic Writings and the somewhat decent The World Needs a Hero. Frankly, of the thrash bands that \\"betrayed\\" the thrash \\"cause\\" in the 1990s - Metallica, Anthrax, and Megadeth all did - Megadeth did the best job of making new formulas work.<br /><br />Fast forward to the 2000s: Where is it written that Megadeth now has to keep the thrash flame alive? Endgame was very good, but, again, Thirteen had less thrashy, but more memorable and higher quality songs. Do you really remember any chorus to any Endgame song besides Headcrusher? But you can both headbang and sing along to Sudden Death, Public Enemy No. 1, and Black Swan. With Super Collider, Megadeth continues to beat out the competition in creativity and quality melodies, if not in thrash. I say kudos.<br /><br />And to all the critics who, while panning Super Collider, mention offhand that it's hard to find good thrash metal these days, I say that's a lie: If you want pure renaissance thrash, try Slayer's superb Christ Illusion, Anthrax's incredible Worship Music, Testament's excellent Dark Roots of Earth, Overkill's great The Electric Age, or Exodus' decent Exhibition B: The Human Condition. If you want angry, aggressive thrash/power metal that kicks you in the face and has you saying thank you, Machine Head's Unto the Locust is by far the best. If you want thrash/power/whatever that hits so hard and has so little melody or variety that you might actually sleep, then Lamb of God is for you. If you want to recall the heavier mainstream metal of the '80s, 3 Inches of Blood's Long Live Heavy Metal is damn near perfect. But if (however secretly) you find thrash-based metal with a pop twist and a Mustaine snarl an attractive guilty pleasure; and if (again, however secretly) you miss that 1990s Countdown to Extinction digestibility or Youthanasia introspection; then look no further than Megedeth's Thirteen and Super Collider. These two albums do not disappoint.

B00BR0SEAK|5.0|The more I'm listening to this album the more I like it. Especially, its more progressive twist which nevertheless is typically laconic and eschews self-indulgent technicality, so common in modern progressive rock. It's eclectic and yet with a cohesive sense of direction. There's even a surf tune \\"Blood From Stone\\", with a strong bass line by Glover. It sounds as if the band sought to summarize their entire career, creating an album with elements from each mark and period. And yet, to me it sounds as a modern version of Mark I, i.e. unexpectedly pre-Gillan era. And as is very appropriate for an album dedicated to the late Jon Lord, keyboards take on marked prominence in the mix, which is in itself very much Mark I. Don Airey really shines here, with Lordesque Hammond work throughout, with classical progressive flights of the organ that would fit well in \\"The Book of Taliesyn\\" (see his intricate, lyrical solo in \\"Uncommon Man\\"), and with some use of synthesizers reminiscent of Lord's experimentation of the Coverdale/Hughes era (compare his solo in \\"Weirdistan\\" to \\"A' 200\\" from \\"Burn\\"). Steve Morse is overall quite restrained, not much in terms of shredding, he has nothing to prove at this stage, but listen to his beautiful legato in \\"Vincent Price\\" or Airey-Morse dueling interplay in \\"Apres Vous\\" â a-la Blackmore-Lord \\"In Rock\\". Gillan's voice is very pleasant indeed when he doesn't try to stretch it out-of-range (and he prudently doesn't on this record). If I'd prefer anything different at all, that would be punchier drums â Paice seems to drown a bit on some of the tracks. Above and Beyond anything else, most of the songs on this album are easily conceivable live (which can hardly be said about their previous few records), with longer solos and all, and I'd really wait for the band to do just that.

0231161166|5.0|I liked that it gives capsule introductions to researchers and takes us into their labs, Most ot the time when I started to lose my grip on a description Ms. Carey would present one of her examples (which some other reviewers found too elementary) and I would get that &#34;click&#34; that would make sense of it. I appreciated the author's strategy of following discoveries through labs and linking the bits together with discoveries by other researchers. I'm sure the presentation in the book is much, much more orderly and progressive than the process felt to those in the middle of it, but I got a little bit of the feeling of scientists transported to an unexpected world (unlike those searching for the expected Higgs Boson, which I'm sure has an excitement all its own) .<br /><br />I said above that this book is not comprehensive or linear. B00BQ1D7X4|4.0|Alice in Chains return just shy of a four year hiatus with their fifth studio album, 2013's [[ASIN:B00BQ1D7X4 The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here]]. Very much characteristic of the band's signature grunge, hard-rocking sound, The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here ends up being nothing short of enjoyable.  The most elite cuts of the effort are incredibly memorable.  The non-standouts aren't shabby either, but often tend to be too repetitive or somewhat one-dimensional in conception.  One thing that is consistent is that the near 70 minute effort certain rocks.<br /><br />\\"Hollow\\" opens capably, establishing the dark, malicious nature that has been associated with the band for years.  Excellent recurrent guitar riffs help to unify musically, while the songwriting stands out, particularly lyric \\"Silence so loud / silence I can't tell my up from down.\\"  Follow-up cut \\"Pretty Done\\" impresses with its `in-your-face' guitars, even if it ends up standing out less than the opener and sports too much repetition.  The chorus definitely captures ones attention (\\"I'm pretty done / once a favored son / I'm pretty done / slap me `round for fun...\\").  \\"Stone\\" is a more notable showing, with aggressive bass and the accessories of the drum kit establishing an evolving groove from the get-go.  A head-nodder, Stone is irresistible, particular simple chorus emphasizes words \\"Cold, dry, stone...\\"<br /><br />\\"Voices\\" opens nebulously, with things eventually establishing themselves (a stable groove).  The main gripe about \\"Voices\\" is it overstays its welcome a minute or so too long.  The lengthier \\"The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here\\" is a actually a stronger, more likable cut, despite approaching seven minutes.  Heavy, noted for its chromatic colorful tones, \\"The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here\\" represents the band at their best.  \\"Lab Monkey\\" keeps the momentum elevated, giving Alice in Chains a second consecutive home-run.  Perversely dark in sound,  you wouldn't think Jerry Cantrell and company were middle-aged rockers.<br /><br />\\"Low Ceiling\\" remains dark, but doesn't sound quite as foreboding as some.  The lyrics still don't scream optimism (\\"Finding ceilings low / I'm too big or this room's too small / why's my ceiling another's floor...\\").  \\"Breath on a Window\\" proceeds  solidly, but like \\"Low Ceiling\\" still falls short of the title track's glory.  \\"Scalpel\\" comes closer (\\"It's a lie, you hide in someone else / like a shining scalpel, cut yourself), even if it doesn't supersede the best.  One positive attribute is that the band `turns down' some, contrasting previous, highly dynamic cuts.  The band returns full throttle on \\"Phantom Limb\\", a seven minute juggernaut rivaling \\"The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here\\".  Jagged/angular riffs, gargantuan sound, and a driving quality help to make it one of the effort's top five.  \\"Ego pulverized / no better medicine / angels have been advised / don't want no requiem\\", the lyrics snarl.  \\"Hung on a Hook\\" and \\"Choke\\" close out the album solidly, but both lack the same punch and vigor of top-echelon knockouts.<br /><br />Ultimately, [[ASIN:B00BQ1D7X4 The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here]] is a great reminder of the magic and power of Alice in Chains. Perhaps nothing will ever supersede \\"Man in the Box\\" or \\"Rooster\\", but standouts the likes of \\"Hollow\\", \\"Stone\\", \\"The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here\\", and \\"Phantom Limb\\" are strong representations from rock royalty.  Highly recommended.kers.<br /><br />\\"Low Ceiling\\" remains dark, but doesn't sound quite as foreboding as some. B00BYISQTO|4.0|Indie-darlings The National deliver once more with their 2013 effort, [[ASIN:B00BYISQTO Trouble Will Find Me]], released via 4AD.  Throughout the solid, enjoyable effort, frontman Matt Berninger's baritone fluctuates between overt clarity and lower-register casualness and nonchalance.  Although sometimes divisive vocally, Berninger certainly adds both magic and character alongside his bandmate's superb musical contributions.<br /><br />\\"I Should Live In Salt\\" establishes a tone, opting against the archetypical 4/4 adding a timing `hiccup' to  distinguish itself.  The different sounds present themselves organically and natural, making the opener convey a strong first impression.  \\"Demons\\" is stronger, even if vocally one is unsure initially where Matt Berninger is going with his gravelly, somewhat unemotional lower register performance.  He eventually ascends, aiding in bring the `demons' full circle.  If nothing more, the chorus is simple (\\"But I stay down with my demons...\\") while lyric (\\"...when I walk into a room I do not light it up...\\" adds indie-comic relief and just a plain, memorable lyric.  If \\"Demons\\" wasn't enough, the five-star worthy \\"Don't Swallow the Cap\\" ensures the listener is mesmerized as Berninger constantly reflects whether it's \\"I'm not alone / I'll never be / and tot eh bone I'm evergreen...\\" or \\"...I have faith but don't believe it / it's not there enough to leave it\\".  \\"Don't Swallow the Cap\\" presents a sound argument as Trouble Will Find Me's top-rated cut.<br /><br />\\"Fireproof\\" may not quite have the `fire' of juggernauts \\"Demons\\" or \\"Don't Swallow the Cap\\", but it continues a strong quality-laden run.  \\"You're fireproof,\\" Berninger sings on the refrain \\"...nothing breaks your heart / you're fireproof.\\"  The restraint of \\"Fireproof\\" is contrasted on single \\"Sea of Love\\", where the dynamics are louder, particularly pummeling drums.  The titular lyric is highlighted (\\"If I stay here, trouble will find me...\\"), though lyrics like \\"...but they say love is a virtue, don't they?\\" or \\"I see you rushing down / what did Harvard teach you?\\" may be more clever, particularly to the indie-lover's heart.  \\"Heavenfaced\\" features appropriate music to match its theme and lyrics, laying as well as anything else does up until this point.  \\"This is The Last Time\\" may be a slight disappointment if one is to be found on Trouble Will Find Me, as it is a bit long and perhaps humdrum compared to the best.<br /><br />\\"Graceless\\" is more notable, establishing a stable groove from the get-go and memorable lyrics throughout.  On \\"Slipped\\", Berninger confesses \\"...I'll never be anything you ever want me to be / I keep coming back here where everything slipped...\\", and while it is `tortured' perhaps, it is quite pleasing vocally, lyrically, and musically to the ear. \\"I Need My Girl\\" has its clever, humorous lines (\\"Remember when you lost your... and drove the car into the garden? You gout out and said I'm sorry to the vines and no one saw it...\\"), but \\"Humiliation\\" is true `the ace in the hole', `chocked full to the brim' with musical excellence.  Penultimate cut \\"Pink Rabbits\\" is a dandy, with clear vocals from Berninger, who asks \\"Am I the one you think about when you're sitting in your fainting chair drinking pink rabbits?\\" \\"Hard to Find\\" closes solidly, but doesn't stand out compared to the top echelon.  Even so, the ambience created by an array of sounds is worthwhile.<br /><br />Ultimately, The National triumph more often than not on [[ASIN:B00BYISQTO Trouble Will Find Me]].  It isn't a perfect sweep, but it's pretty doggone good.  There is more than enough enjoyment to be had.  It may not eclipse Vampire Weekend's juggernaut in [[ASIN:B00BB22GQM Modern Vampires of the City]] (arguably the year's best alternative album), but it definitely one of 2013's better albums.y say love is a virtue, don't they?\\" or \\"I see you rushing down / what did Harvard teach you?\\" may be more clever, particularly to the indie-lover's heart.  \\"Heavenfaced\\" features appropriate music to match its theme and lyrics, laying as well as anything else does up until this point.  \\"This is The Last Time\\" may be a slight disappointment if one is to be found on Trouble Will Find Me, as it is a bit long and perhaps humdrum compared to the best.<br /><br />\\"Graceless\\" is more notable, establishing a stable groove from the get-go and memorable lyrics throughout. B00C1N9JWO|5.0|--------No Spoilers Ahead ----------<br />One of my biggest fears in any series that have ten or more books in it, is that the story line becomes exhausted and all you get are a formula romances where everything is predictable. Repeatable even. It is not the case with Nalini Singh.<br /><br />I think all the books before this were necessary for the Psy/Changeling world to come to a place where this was possible. I won't mention the details, as many of the people are yet to read, but it's suffice to say - the book is explosive.<br /><br />It's not just romance that drives it; it's not a case of few, predictable events generated simply to highlight the romance part. The overall plot of the series (which picks up speed immensely in this book) works hand in hand with the love story one furthering another to the point where a reader is sitting on the edge of his seat desperate to find out what happens next.<br /><br />And there are many things to find out, many things that will leave you speechless and awestruck. Many details that make Heart of Obsidian into one of the most thrilling, intense rides of your life.<br /><br />I mean any author who could turn a character such as Kaleb into a likable, relatable lead without destroying what he is, without changing him fundamentally, must be a genius. She manages everything nicely: lets him stay true to himself and gives us a glimpse into the darkest corners of his soul. Furthermore, Nalini Singh pens Kaleb and Sahara as a couple it's not possible not to adore. Because despite of their flaws, they complete each other.<br /><br />I must admit I waited for this book so much, I paid 18 bucks for my kindle edition just to put my hands on it as soon as possible. And while the amount of money is actually preposterous for an e-book (especially considering that the same e-book costs 7 bucks less if bought two days later) I'm so thrilled with the read I don't even care about the extra costs. Because lets face it, most books that you wait for so long, and built enormous expectations not even trying to, let you down. But not Heart of Obsidian.<br /><br />It has everything: a breathtaking romance, suspense, the drama and action you won't believe until you read it. I don't think I can stress enough how much I loved it. All I can say - it'll spin your world upside down before you even knew what hit you.<br />Heart of Obsidian will be very, very hard to top. It took me a while, but if you can separate the book from the film in your mind it will haunt you in a whole new way.<br /><br />There are literally hundreds of plot summaries of \\"The Shining,\\" so I will dispense with that for the purposes of this review. I will point out that where the book really shines (ahem) is in its grittiness and in its history of the hotel. King gets downright nasty in the book with his unsparing descriptions of Jack's alcoholic self-destruction. We get into Jack's head and see the dark, twisted logic that begins to take hold in the corners of his mind. We get into Doc's and Dick Hallorann's heads to understand their gifts more fully. Most importantly, King uses the scrapbook of the Overlook's history to help get us into the hotel's \\"mind\\" and how it has grown its power and menace steadily since the turn of the century.<br /><br />The Kubrick film is excellent in its own right, but it really is a separate story written mostly by Stanley Kubrick himself with inspiration from Stephen King. The TV miniseries was more faithful to the book but the acting by Rebecca De Mornay, Steven Weber, and Melvin Van Peebles is roll-your-eyes cheesy at times. Stephen King's book is the first and best version. Nowhere else do you get the full psychological isolation and terror that makes \\"The Shining\\" so special.<br /><br />Clear your mind of both filmed versions, open the book, and begin anew like I did. Don't let anyone else's interpretation get between you and Stephen King's twisted whispering going directly into your ear on a long, dark, sleepless night. If you want the full \\"Shining\\" experience, that's the way to do it.

B008B1BL4E|4.0|Six little boys tussle in a sandbox, pushing and shoving, sometimes openly, sometimes when none of the others are looking. One of them, a runt, is getting the worst of it, but he's a vicious little guy and manages to hold his own within his own tiny corner of the sandbox. The biggest boys exert the least effort but command the most space. They all look confident, but secretly they're terrified of one another, leading them to combine forces in a constantly shifting pattern of partnerships to fend off the others.<br /><br />This is the image that comes to mind of Europe in the summer of 1914 from reading Christopher Clark's new inquiry into how the First World War came to be. Naturally, Professor Clark had something much more grown-up in mind when he wrote the book. After all, he is a Fellow at St. Catherine's College at the University of Cambridge, where he received his Ph.D. in History, and we all know that a Cambridge Don would never indulge in such belittling imagery.<br /><br />In all fairness, to put the event in proper perspective, \\"The conflict that began that summer mobilized 65 million troops, claimed three empires [Russian, Ottoman, and Austro-Hungarian], 20 million military and civilian deaths, and 21 million wounded. The horrors of Europe's twentieth century were born of this catastrophe.\\"<br /><br />With The Sleepwalkers, Clark muscles into the seemingly endless debate about why and how all this came to pass. Not that anybody on the street is talking about this stuff, of course. But among modern European historians these questions pass for excitement, and no wonder: the Great War is generally taken as the climax of the well-ordered Victorian Era that launched the human race with a lurch into the 20th Century. The origins of the cataclysm that upended tens of millions of lives are variously found in Prussian militarism, the colliding interests of European empires, the arms race, the profit motive among arms merchants, and other cross-border phenomena, but Professor Clark apparently will have none of this. He's a practitioner of that brand of history that finds truth in the quotidian details of human interaction -- in short, in the day-to-day decisions of living, breathing human beings tossed together in a crisis that nobody foresaw.<br /><br />In the first of its three parts, The Sleepwalkers thus explores the political environment, highlighting the major players in each of the contending nations -- Serbia, Austro-Hungary, Russia, Germany, France, and England -- in the years running up to 1914. Part II takes a broader look at the Continent, discussing the interplay of the leading states in the closing years of the 19th Century and the beginning of the 20th. In outline, the stable alliances of the late 1880s had given way to a bipolar system by 1907, with the Triple Alliance of Germany, Austria-Hungary, and (loosely) Italy facing off against the interlocking fortunes of Russia, France, and Great Britain. Clark asserts that \\"[t]he polarization of Europe's geopolitical system was a crucial precondition for the war that broke out in 1914.\\" Then, in Part III, Clark delves deeply into the day-by-day, sometimes hour-by-hour, decisions of the leading players from June 28, when Gavrilo Princip shot to death the heir to the Austro-Hungarian throne and his wife, until the early days of August, when all the chips had fallen into place and war was declared on all fronts.<br /><br />In Clark's view, \\"1914 is less remote from us -- less illegible -- now than it was in the 1980s. Since the end of the Cold War, a system of bipolar stability has made way for a more complex and unpredictable array of forces, including declining empires and rising powers -- a state of affairs that invites comparison with the Europe of 1914.\\" Although Clark makes it easy to see history repeating itself in small ways -- for example, the genocidal course pursued by Serbia in the 1990s was little different from its behavior in the decades leading up to 1914 -- it's difficult to see the parallels to most of today's international crises. Surely, Professor Clark wouldn't pretend that the U.S. invasion of Iraq -- one of the seminal events of our times and perhaps the greatest strategic blunder in American history -- was anything but the result of hubris and colossal miscalculation on the part of an ideology-driven clique within the U.S. government.<br /><br />Disagreements aside, however, The Sleepwalkers is an outstanding piece of work.  Professor Clark's knowledge of the period he writes about is both broad and deep, and he writes with grace and verve that's highly unusual in academic circles.4 -- it's difficult to see the parallels to most of today's international crises. B005ZOBNOI|5.0|Warning! Spoilers in comment! Don't read this if you haven't read the book.<br /><br />At first I didn't want to read this book because I knew that someone was going to die and someone was going to lose someone and etc. In fact, it took me about a month to even convince myself to buy it for my kindle, but I kept putting it off with other stories about how the girl gets the guy or how someone saves this magical land. Some fantasy that always had a happy beginning, middle, and end. I had been afraid to experience, once again, the curse of an attachment to someone with terminal disease such as cancer. In stories like these, someone always dies. And it's usually the healthy person. And it usually teaches us as regular human beings some universal lesson to love each other, keep promises, etc etc. John Green captured precisely all that ran through my mind around the time of my Aunts' deaths, one having died of bone cancer and the other of breast and stomach cancer. And the same when my Grandfather passed away less than a year ago. I don't know why we as humans always complain about dealing with pain as it is very unpleasant (for us and for those around us. No one wants to have to be the consoler or consolee), but yet we subject ourselves to it all the time through stories and the need to feel. When I began reading this book, it was hard because I would remember my aunt having the same mindset as Augustus, and I would remember the countless hours of trying to figure out life and death, which still proved to benefit me in no way as I was not and never will be prepared for death. I believe they are no longer feeling pain, so I'm happy, but I'm still feeling pain, so in a way my mourning is selfish. Anyway, I couldn't get through without crying and missing who I've lost and coming back around to realize, just like Hayley Grace, that we are grenades waiting to hurt someone, even without meaning to, so I don't want to die and be that grenade. I've always believed that life wasmade as a source of happiness for other people. You are something someone else loves, which is why I feel some people have such a hard time loving themselves, yet are so afraid to die. We are made to care about other people, and would rather not put grief upon others so why would dying be any different. Anyway, I read the book of course, as pain is only an obstacle and a benefit to the overall message and heartwarming tale that is this book. I would just like to say that John Green's mind is genius and understanding. There are few people who can claim to understand the misguided truths and fates of the living, but he does so with dignity and confidence AND for the world to see, which is why this, so far, is the best book I've ever read.

B000002GQH|5.0|The Moody Blues are an entirely unique and masterful band, embodying the the finest aesthetic of that era. As 14 year old boy, in 1969, I chanced to hear the debut of Threshold of a Dream on our local radio station in Anchorage, AK. I was totally blown away, because it went inside me and made me imagine and think things. It wasn't just a good beat and guitar lines. Being very imaginative and spiritually inclined, as well as a burgeoning rock guitarist, I had felt, and still do, that the Moody Blues are not only musically unique and amazing, but also able to express real spiritual and metaphysical ideas and emotions without appearing corny or contriving. When these guys sing that people in the world need to love each other, it has the profound reverence and sincere longing such a statement should have. When they suggest you think of something, it's usually mysterious, adventurous, joyful, fraught with danger, loss, or sorrow. The Moody Blues are able to speak to the inner world as well as the outer with their incredible talents. Sure, there seem to always be three or so cuts from every album that I don't think are so great, but the rest are such beautiful masterpieces that each will play in my mind and soul until I go on.<br /><br />Read all the wonderful reviews that speak so eloquently to the quality of their music and the appreciated talents of all it's band members/song writers.<br /><br />But a hint to those unfamiliar with the Moody's musical catalog, the NEXT album is the Moody Blues crowning achievement. To Our Children's Children's Children is astounding, and the remastered version here on Amazon is the mix that should have originally been released. When TOCCC came out, it was a distorted mix. Now it is clean. Same incredible stupendous music, but CLEAN. Plus bonus tracks of course! I cannot praise TOCCC enough, it is an AWESOME achievement, and deserves to be heard at least for the next four generations! For me, Seventh Sojourn was the last Moody Blues album I could say I liked. They really burned bright, so I'm glad they were able to produce as much incredible music as they did. I hope they all take satisfaction in having given so many people the inspiration they have.

Even that one cost $25 million and grossed a little over $50 million so it just cut it. And \\"Michael Clayton\\" is a damned good picture in my book.<br /><br />\\"John Carter\\" is a grand adventure in the old science fiction/fantasy tradition -- what we referred to as \\"space opera\\" years ago. The story by Edgar Rice Burroughs is only one of a series of stories with this character. At the beginning, John Carter is a deserter from the Confederate Army who is captured by Union soldiers. He makes an escape, hides in a cave and sees strange markings and a medallion in the hand of a dying man. He is holding the medallion as he repeats a word being muttered by the man: \\"Barsoom.\\" He is immediately transported to the planet Mars, the name of which is \\"Barsoom\\" to the Martians. The story takes off from there. The visual effects are state of the art, although the story is a bit muddled and unclear as to who is fighting whom.  The quality of acting is on a level superior to at least two of the Star Wars films (episodes 1 and 2)  It is grand entertainment in the old space opera tradition. A friend of mine who knew about the Burroughs story used to joke that when he retired he would move to Mars and open a saloon there called the Barsoom Bar Room.<br /><br />I did enjoy the movie which is visually quite impressive. It is interesting that the title given at the beginning is simply \\"John Carter.\\" At the end, at the start of the credit roll, the title is given as \\"John Carter of Mars.\\" That was the original title but, for some reason, \\"of Mars.\\" was dropped. Whether the original title would have helped sell the picture, well, as they say in the business, that's blood under the bridge. Apparently there were plans for a series of movies based on the John Carter stories. I think we can forget about that.

B00008LKDY|5.0|Vivaldi published so many violin concerti, and there are so many recordings of them, that it seem hard to imagine that any particular opus or recording might really stick in your memory. This is one of them.<br /><br />Anyone who fears to hear the scratchy, raw sound of a second-rate or older school baroque violinist needs not worry. Rachel Podger's sound is clear, full and consistent. Her amazing technical facility allow her to extemporize more than even some of the foremost virtuosi. To me her playing is right up there in terms of sheer beauty with Manze on his Corelli, or maybe Chandler on his Vivaldi discs with La Serenissima.<br /><br />In addition to La Serenissima, the Polish baroque ensemble Arte dei Suonatori boasts stellar records of Telemann, Handel and Vivaldi, among others. This is their best disc, in my opinion. Their fast Vivaldi playing is in-your-face, percussive, always driving. The slow movements are always gripping, heart-wrenching, though never upstaging Podger, who unquesrtionably dominates these movements, as the solo violin ought to in the slow movement of any Vivaldi violin concerto.<br /><br />This recording received a Gramophone award for \\"Best Baroque instrumental\\" in 2003, and anyone who chooses to give it a try will almost certainly be satisfied.

B00B7364H2|5.0|When I was growing up my family had an RCA Orthophonic Hi-Fi that could play LPs, 45s, and even 78s by flipping the stylus over via a little lever on the tonearm. Nobody in the family was particularly musical save me -- I went on to a career in music -- but we enjoyed music. I was the only one in the family to make the move to stereo, courtesy of my own RCA stereo record player that I got sometime around 1966 or so, as I recall, as a Christmas present.<br /><br />In those days newer was always better. I couldn't start acquiring stereo records until I had my stereo record player, after all, and once I could, monophonic seemed old-hat and passÃ©. So new was always the way to go. The re-issue stuff on labels like RCA Camden, Columbia Odyssey, and London Stereo Treasury was mostly for times when funds were low, although there were some real treasures tucked into those record jackets.<br /><br />But we're in a different time now, when the treasures of the past are more enticing. I'm delighted that the major labels have seen fit to give such attention to their back catalogs, especially when I consider an alternate reality in which the labels let everything languish until the master tapes had decayed past full revival.<br /><br />So at some level I don't think it matters whether the performances are middle-of-the-road or exemplary, or whether the recorded sound is overly bright or too close or whatnot. The critical thing, to my mind, is that the recordings were saved. True, somewhere down the road they'll need to be re-issued via whatever storage means become the future norm. But those original magnetic tapes won't last forever, and the original LPs are subject to wear & tear, loss, and the like. In short, we could lose portions of our recorded heritage. These box-set reissues, and the remastering that went into creating them, have bought us a big chunk of time.<br /><br />The labels themselves own the copyrights to these recordings and can therefore do with them what theywill; someday the recordings will pass into public domain but will the original tapes be in viable condition? (And the labels will continue to own the physical tapes no matter what their copyright status.) So I remain deeply grateful for these box set releases, not only Mercury, but RCA and EMI and Deutsche Grammophon and Decca and all those EMI sets and so on and so forth. I have a soft spot for them and snap them up whenever they come out; I don't want to miss any, and it comforts me to know precisely where I can put my hands on well preserved copies of so much of our recorded heritage: I need look no further than my own library.
